scope="row">Manager</th><td>\nVacant</td></tr><tr><th scope="row">General Manager</th><td>\n<a href="/wiki/Thad_Levine" title="Thad Levine">Thad Levine</a><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="/wiki/Derek_Falvey" title="Derek Falvey">Derek Falvey</a><sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The <b>Minnesota Twins</b> are an American professional baseball team based in <a href="/wiki/Minneapolis" title="Minneapolis">Minneapolis, Minnesota</a>. The Twins compete in <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball" title="Major League Baseball">Major League Baseball</a> (MLB) as a member club of the <a href="/wiki/American_League" title="American League">American League</a> (AL) <a href="/wiki/American_League_Central" title="American League Central">Central</a> division. The team is named after the <a href="/wiki/Minneapolis%E2%80%93Saint_Paul" title="Minneapolis\xe2\x80\x93Saint Paul">Twin Cities</a> area comprising Minneapolis and <a href="/wiki/Saint_Paul,_Minnesota" title="Saint Paul, Minnesota">St. Paul</a>. They played in <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Stadium" title="Metropolitan Stadium">Metropolitan Stadium</a> from 1961 to 1981 and the <a href="/wiki/Hubert_H._Humphrey_Metrodome" title="Hubert H. Humphrey Metrodome">Hubert H. Humphrey Metrodome</a> from 1982 to 2009. They played their inaugural game at the newly completed <a href="/wiki/Target_Field" title="Target Field">Target Field</a> on April 12, 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The team was founded in <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a> in <a href="/wiki/1901_Major_League_Baseball_season" title="1901 Major League Baseball season">1901</a> as one of the eight original teams of the American League, named the <b><a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Washington_Senators_(1901%E2%80%9360)" title="History of the Washington Senators (1901\xe2\x80\x9360)">Washington Senators</a></b> or <b>Washington Nationals</b> (both names had been used in the clubs early years and no official name was used thereafter). Although the Washington team endured long bouts of mediocrity (immortalized in the 1955 Broadway musical <i><a href="/wiki/Damn_Yankees" title="Damn Yankees">Damn Yankees</a></i>),<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> they had a period of prolonged success in the 1920s and 1930s, led by Baseball Hall of Fame members <a href="/wiki/Bucky_Harris" title="Bucky Harris">Bucky Harris</a>, <a href="/wiki/Goose_Goslin" title="Goose Goslin">Goose Goslin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sam_Rice" title="Sam Rice">Sam Rice</a>, <a href="/wiki/Heinie_Manush" title="Heinie Manush">Heinie Manush</a>, <a href="/wiki/Joe_Cronin" title="Joe Cronin">Joe Cronin</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Walter_Johnson" title="Walter Johnson">Walter Johnson</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Manager <a href="/wiki/Clark_Griffith" title="Clark Griffith">Clark Griffith</a> joined the team in 1912 and became the team\'s owner in 1920.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> The franchise remained under Griffith family ownership until 1984.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1960, Major League Baseball granted the city of Minneapolis an expansion team. Washington owner <a href="/wiki/Calvin_Griffith" title="Calvin Griffith">Calvin Griffith</a>, Clark\'s nephew and adopted son, requested that he be allowed to <a href="/wiki/Relocation_of_professional_sports_teams" title="Relocation of professional sports teams">move</a> his team to <a href="/wiki/Minneapolis-St._Paul" class="mw-redirect" title="Minneapolis-St. Paul">Minneapolis-St. Paul</a> and instead give Washington the expansion team. Upon league approval, the team moved to <a href="/wiki/Minnesota" title="Minnesota">Minnesota</a> after the 1960 season, setting up shop in <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Stadium" title="Metropolitan Stadium">Metropolitan Stadium</a>, while Washington fielded a brand new "Washington Senators" (which later became the <a href="/wiki/Texas_Rangers_(baseball)" title="Texas Rangers (baseball)">Texas Rangers</a> prior to the 1972 season).<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Success came quickly to the team in Minnesota. Sluggers <a href="/wiki/Harmon_Killebrew" title="Harmon Killebrew">Harmon Killebrew</a><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Bob_Allison" title="Bob Allison">Bob Allison</a>, who had already been stars in Washington, were joined by <a href="/wiki/Tony_Oliva" title="Tony Oliva">Tony Oliva</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zoilo_Versalles" title="Zoilo Versalles">Zoilo Versalles</a>, and later <a href="/wiki/Second_baseman" title="Second baseman">second baseman</a> <a href="/wiki/Rod_Carew" title="Rod Carew">Rod Carew</a><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> and pitchers <a href="/wiki/Jim_Kaat" title="Jim Kaat">Jim Kaat</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jim_Perry_(baseball)" title="Jim Perry (baseball)">Jim Perry</a>, winning the <a href="/wiki/List_of_American_League_pennant_winners" title="List of American League pennant winners">American League pennant</a> in 1965.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> A second wave of success came in the late 1980s and early 1990s under manager <a href="/wiki/Tom_Kelly_(baseball)" title="Tom Kelly (baseball)">Tom Kelly</a>, led by <a href="/wiki/Kent_Hrbek" title="Kent Hrbek">Kent Hrbek</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bert_Blyleven" title="Bert Blyleven">Bert Blyleven</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Frank_Viola" title="Frank Viola">Frank Viola</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Kirby_Puckett" title="Kirby Puckett">Kirby Puckett</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> winning the franchise\'s second and third World Series (and first and second in Minnesota).<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Through the 2017 season, the franchise has won three World Series championships (1924, 1987, and 1991), and has fielded 18 <a href="/wiki/List_of_Major_League_Baseball_batting_champions" title="List of Major League Baseball batting champions">American League batting champions</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> The team has hosted five <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball_All-Star_Game" title="Major League Baseball All-Star Game">All-Star Games</a> (1937, 1956, 1965, 1985 and 2014).\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#Washington_Nationals/Senators:_1901\xe2\x80\x931960"><span class="tocnumber">1.1</span> <span class="toctext">Washington Nationals/Senators: 1901\xe2\x80\x931960</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-3"><a href="#Team_nickname"><span class="tocnumber">1.1.1</span> <span class="toctext">Team nickname</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-4"><a href="#Minnesota_Twins:_1961_to_present"><span class="tocnumber">1.2</span> <span class="toctext">Minnesota Twins: 1961 to present</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-5"><a class="toclevel-4 tocsection-9"><a href="#2006"><span class="tocnumber">1.2.4.1</span> <span class="toctext">2006</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-4 tocsection-10"><a href="#2008"><span class="tocnumber">1.2.4.2</span> <span class="toctext">2008</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-4 tocsection-11"><a href="#2009"><span class="tocnumber">1.2.4.3</span> <span tocsection-13"><a href="#2011"><span class="tocnumber">1.2.4.5</span> <span class="toctext">2011</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-4 tocsection-14"><a href="#2012\xe2\x80\x932016"><span class="tocnumber">1.2.4.6</span> <span class="toctext">2012\xe2\x80\x932016</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-15"><a href="#Threatened_contraction_or_relocation_of_the_team"><span class="tocnumber">1.3</span> <span class="toctext">Threatened contraction or relocation of the team</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-16"><a href="#Target_Field"><span class="tocnumber">1.4</span> <span class="toctext">Target Field</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-17"><a href="#Current_roster"><span class="tocnumber">2</span> <span class="toctext">Current roster</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-18"><a href="#Minor_league_affiliates"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Minor league affiliates</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-19"><a href="#Achievements"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Achievements</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-20"><a href="#Baseball_Hall_of_Famers"><span class="tocnumber">4.1</span> <span class="toctext">Baseball Hall of Famers</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-21"><a href="#Ford_C._Frick_Award_recipients"><span class="tocnumber">4.2</span> <span class="toctext">Ford C. Frick Award recipients</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-22"><a href="#Team_captains"><span class="tocnumber">4.3</span> <span class="toctext">Team captains</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-23"><a href="#Twins_Hall_of_Fame"><span class="tocnumber">4.4</span> <span class="toctext">Twins Hall of Fame</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-24"><a href="#Retired_numbers"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Retired numbers</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-25"><a href="#Awards"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Awards</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-26"><a href="#Team_records"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Team records</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-27"><a href="#Team_seasons"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Team seasons</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-28"><a href="#Radio_and_television"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Radio and television</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-29"><a href="#Community_activities"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Community activities</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-30"><a href="#Team_and_franchise_traditions"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">Team and franchise traditions</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-31"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-32"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-33"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-34"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">15</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Team_history">Team history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Minnesota_Twins&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Team history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Minnesota_Twins" title="History of the Minnesota Twins">History of the Minnesota Twins</a></div>\n<h3><span id="Washington_Nationals.2FSenators:_1901.E2.80.931960"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Washington_Nationals/Senators:_1901\xe2\x80\x931960">Washington Nationals/Senators: 1901\xe2\x80\x931960</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Minnesota_Twins&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Washington Nationals/Senators: 1901\xe2\x80\x931960">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Washington_Senators" class="mw-redirect" title="History of the Washington Senators">History of the Washington Senators</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1924worldseries.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/1924worldseries.jpg/300px-1924worldseries.jpg" width="300" height="125" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/1924worldseries.jpg/450px-1924worldseries.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/1924worldseries.jpg/600px-1924worldseries.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3706" data-file-height="1545" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1924worldseries.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Washington\'s <a href="/wiki/Bucky_Harris" title="Bucky Harris">Bucky Harris</a> scores on his home run in the fourth inning of Game 7 of the 1924 World Series.</div></div></div>\n<p>The Washington Senators spent the first decade of their existence finishing near the bottom of the American League standings. Their fortunes began to improve with the arrival of 19-year-old pitcher, Walter Johnson, in <a href="/wiki/1907_Washington_Senators_season" title="1907 Washington Senators season">1907</a>. Johnson blossomed in <a href="/wiki/1911_Washington_Senators_season" title="1911 Washington Senators season">1911</a> with 25 victories, although the Senators still finished the season in seventh place.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/1912_Washington_Senators_season" title="1912 Washington Senators season">1912</a>, the Senators improved dramatically, as their pitching staff led the league in team <a href="/wiki/Earned_run_average" title="Earned run average">earned run average</a> and in <a href="/wiki/Strikeout" title="Strikeout">strikeouts</a>. Johnson won 33 games while teammate <a href="/wiki/Bob_Groom" title="Bob Groom">Bob Groom</a> added another 24 wins to help the Senators finish the season in second place.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> The Senators continued to perform respectably in <a href="/wiki/1913_Washington_Senators_season" title="1913 Washington Senators season">1913</a> with Johnson posting a career-high 35 victories, as the team once again finished in second place.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> The Senators then fell into another period of decline for the next decade. After a string of mediocre seasons, a rejuvenated Johnson rebounded in <a href="/wiki/1924_Washington_Senators_season" title="1924 Washington Senators season">1924</a> to win 23 games with the help of his catcher, <a href="/wiki/Muddy_Ruel" title="Muddy Ruel">Muddy Ruel</a>, as the Senators won the <a href="/wiki/List_of_American_League_pennant_winners" title="List of American League pennant winners">American League pennant</a> for the first time in the history of the franchise.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Senators faced <a href="/wiki/John_McGraw" title="John McGraw">John McGraw</a>\'s heavily favored <a href="/wiki/1924_New_York_Giants_season" title="1924 New York Giants season">New York Giants</a> in the <a href="/wiki/1924_World_Series" title="1924 World Series">1924 World Series</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> The two teams traded wins back and forth until the series reached the seventh and deciding game. The Senators trailed the Giants 3 to 1 in the eighth inning of Game 7, when Bucky Harris hit a routine ground ball to third which hit a pebble and took a bad hop over Giants third baseman <a href="/wiki/Freddie_Lindstrom" title="Freddie Lindstrom">Freddie Lindstrom</a>. Two runners scored on the play, tying the score at three.<sup id="cite_ref-1924_World_Series_Game_7_box_score_at_Baseball_Reference_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1924_World_Series_Game_7_box_score_at_Baseball_Reference-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> An aging Walter Johnson then came in to pitch the ninth inning, and held the Giants scoreless into extra innings. In the bottom of the twelfth inning with Ruel at bat, he hit a high, <a href="/wiki/Foul_ball" title="Foul ball">foul ball</a> directly over <a href="/wiki/Home_plate" class="mw-redirect" title="Home plate">home plate</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-How_Senators&#39;_Strategy_Won_for_Johnson_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-How_Senators&#39;_Strategy_Won_for_Johnson-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> The Giants\' catcher, <a href="/wiki/Hank_Gowdy" title="Hank Gowdy">Hank Gowdy</a>, dropped his protective mask to field the ball but, failing to toss the mask aside, stumbled over it and dropped the ball, thus giving Ruel another chance to bat.<sup id="cite_ref-How_Senators&#39;_Strategy_Won_for_Johnson_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-How_Senators&#39;_Strategy_Won_for_Johnson-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> On the next <a href="/wiki/Pitch_(baseball)" title="Pitch (baseball)">pitch</a>, Ruel hit a <a href="/wiki/Double_(baseball)" title="Double (baseball)">double</a> and proceeded to score the winning <a href="/wiki/Run_(baseball)" title="Run (baseball)">run</a> when <a href="/wiki/Earl_McNeely" title="Earl McNeely">Earl McNeely</a> hit a <a href="/wiki/Ground_ball" class="mw-redirect" title="Ground ball">ground ball</a> that took another bad hop over Lindstrom\'s head.<sup id="cite_ref-1924_World_Series_Game_7_box_score_at_Baseball_Reference_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1924_World_Series_Game_7_box_score_at_Baseball_Reference-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-How_Senators&#39;_Strategy_Won_for_Johnson_28-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-How_Senators&#39;_Strategy_Won_for_Johnson-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> This would mark the only <a href="/wiki/World_Series" title="World Series">World Series</a> triumph for the franchise during their 60-year tenure in Washington.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/1925_Major_League_Baseball_season" title="1925 Major League Baseball season">following season</a> they repeated as <a href="/wiki/American_League" title="American League">American League</a> champions but ultimately lost the <a href="/wiki/1925_World_Series" title="1925 World Series">1925 World Series</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Pittsburgh_Pirates" title="Pittsburgh Pirates">Pittsburgh Pirates</a>. After Walter Johnson\'s retirement in 1927, he was hired as manager of the Senators. After enduring a few losing seasons, the team returned to contention in 1930. In <a href="/wiki/1933_Washington_Senators_season" title="1933 Washington Senators season">1933</a>, Senators owner <a href="/wiki/Clark_Griffith" title="Clark Griffith">Clark Griffith</a> returned to the formula that worked for him nine years prior: 26-year-old shortstop Joe Cronin became <a href="/wiki/Player-manager" class="mw-redirect" title="Player-manager">player-manager</a>. The Senators posted a 99\xe2\x80\x9353 record and cruised to the pennant seven games ahead of the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Yankees" title="New York Yankees">New York Yankees</a>, but in the <a href="/wiki/1933_World_Series" title="1933 World Series">1933 World Series</a> the Giants exacted their revenge winning in five games. Following the loss, the Senators sank all the way to seventh place in <a href="/wiki/1934_Washington_Senators_season" title="1934 Washington Senators season">1934</a> and attendance began to fall. Despite the return of Harris as manager from 1935\xe2\x80\x9342 and again from 1950\xe2\x80\x9354, Washington was mostly a losing ball club for the next 25 years contending for the pennant only during World War II. Washington came to be known as "first in war, first in peace, and last in the American League",<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> with their hard luck being crucial to the plot of the musical and film <i><a href="/wiki/Damn_Yankees_(musical)" class="mw-redirect" title="Damn Yankees (musical)">Damn Yankees</a></i>. <a href="/wiki/Cecil_Travis" title="Cecil Travis">Cecil Travis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Buddy_Myer" title="Buddy Myer">Buddy Myer</a> (<a href="/wiki/List_of_Major_League_Baseball_Batting_Champions" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Major League Baseball Batting Champions">1935 A.L. batting champion</a>), <a href="/wiki/Roy_Sievers" title="Roy Sievers">Roy Sievers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mickey_Vernon" title="Mickey Vernon">Mickey Vernon</a> (<a href="/wiki/List_of_Major_League_Baseball_Batting_Champions" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Major League Baseball Batting Champions">batting champion in 1946 and 1953</a>), and <a href="/wiki/Eddie_Yost" title="Eddie Yost">Eddie Yost</a> were notable Senators players whose careers were spent in obscurity due to the team\'s lack of success.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/1954_Washington_Senators_season" title="1954 Washington Senators season">1954</a>, the Senators signed future Hall of Fame member <a href="/wiki/Harmon_Killebrew" title="Harmon Killebrew">Harmon Killebrew</a>. By <a href="/wiki/1959_Washington_Senators_season" title="1959 Washington Senators season">1959</a> he was the Senators\xe2\x80\x99 regular third baseman and led the league with 42 home runs earning him a starting spot on the <a href="/wiki/1959_Major_League_Baseball_All-Star_Game_(first_game)" title="1959 Major League Baseball All-Star Game (first game)">American League All-Star team</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Walter_Johnson_and_Calvin_Coolidge_shake_hands_FINAL.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Walter_Johnson_and_Calvin_Coolidge_shake_hands_FINAL.jpg/300px-Walter_Johnson_and_Calvin_Coolidge_shake_hands_FINAL.jpg" width="300" height="124" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Walter_Johnson_and_Calvin_Coolidge_shake_hands_FINAL.jpg/450px-Walter_Johnson_and_Calvin_Coolidge_shake_hands_FINAL.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Walter_Johnson_and_Calvin_Coolidge_shake_hands_FINAL.jpg/600px-Walter_Johnson_and_Calvin_Coolidge_shake_hands_FINAL.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4549" data-file-height="1873" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Walter_Johnson_and_Calvin_Coolidge_shake_hands_FINAL.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>President <a href="/wiki/Calvin_Coolidge" title="Calvin Coolidge">Calvin Coolidge</a> (left) and Washington Senators pitcher <a href="/wiki/Walter_Johnson" title="Walter Johnson">Walter Johnson</a> (right) shake hands following the Senators\' 1924 championship.</div></div></div>\n<p>After Griffith\'s death in 1955, his nephew and adopted son <a href="/wiki/Calvin_Griffith" title="Calvin Griffith">Calvin</a> took over the team presidency. Calvin sold <a href="/wiki/Griffith_Stadium" title="Griffith Stadium">Griffith Stadium</a> to the city of Washington and leased it back leading to speculation that the team was planning to move as the <a href="/wiki/Boston_Braves_(baseball)" class="mw-redirect" title="Boston Braves (baseball)">Boston Braves</a>, <a href="/wiki/St._Louis_Browns" class="mw-redirect" title="St. Louis Browns">St. Louis Browns</a> and <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_Athletics" class="mw-redirect" title="Philadelphia Athletics">Philadelphia Athletics</a> had all done in the early 1950s. By 1957, after an early flirtation with San Francisco (where the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Giants_(NL)" class="mw-redirect" title="New York Giants (NL)">New York Giants</a> would eventually move after that season ended), Griffith began courting Minneapolis\xe2\x80\x93St. Paul, a prolonged process that resulted in his rejecting the Twin Cities\' first offer<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> before agreeing to relocate. The American League opposed the move at first, but in 1960 a deal was reached: The Senators would move and would be replaced with an expansion <a href="/wiki/Texas_Rangers_(baseball)" title="Texas Rangers (baseball)">Senators</a> team for 1961. Thus, the old Washington Senators became the Minnesota Twins.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Team_nickname">Team nickname</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Minnesota_Twins&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Team nickname">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The Washington franchise was known as both "Senators" and "Nationals" at various times, and sometimes at the same time.  In 1905, the team changed its official name to the "Washington Nationals."<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> The name "Nationals" appeared on uniforms for only two seasons, and was then replaced with the "W" logo for the next 52 years. The media often shortened the nickname to "Nats." Many fans and newspapers (especially out-of-town papers) persisted in using the "Senators" nickname, because of potential confusion caused by an American League team using the "Nationals" name. Over time, "Nationals" faded as a nickname, and "Senators" became dominant. Baseball guides listed the club\'s nickname as "Nationals or Senators", acknowledging the dual-nickname situation.\n</p><p>The team name was officially changed to Washington Senators around the time of <a href="/wiki/Clark_Griffith" title="Clark Griffith">Clark Griffith</a>\'s death. It was not until 1959 that the word "Senators" first appeared on team shirts. "Nats" continued to be used by space-saving headline writers, even for the <a href="/wiki/Texas_Rangers_(baseball)" title="Texas Rangers (baseball)">1961 expansion team</a>, which was never officially known as "Nationals."\n</p><p>The current "Nationals" and "Nats" names were revived in 2005, when the <a href="/wiki/Montreal_Expos" title="Montreal Expos">Montreal Expos</a> relocated to Washington to become the <a href="/wiki/Washington_Nationals" title="Washington Nationals">Nationals</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Minnesota_Twins:_1961_to_present">Minnesota Twins: 1961 to present</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Minnesota_Twins&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Minnesota Twins: 1961 to present">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The name "Twins" was derived from the popular name of the region, the Twin Cities (<a href="/wiki/Minneapolis" title="Minneapolis">Minneapolis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Saint_Paul,_Minnesota" title="Saint Paul, Minnesota">St. Paul</a>). The <a href="/wiki/National_Basketball_Association" title="National Basketball Association">NBA</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Minneapolis_Lakers" class="mw-redirect" title="Minneapolis Lakers">Minneapolis Lakers</a> had relocated to Los Angeles in 1960 due to poor attendance which was believed to have been caused in part by the reluctance of fans in St. Paul to support the team.<sup id="cite_ref-twinstrivia.com_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twinstrivia.com-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Griffith was determined not to alienate fans in either city by naming the team after one city or the other, so his desire was to name the team the "Twin Cities Twins",<sup id="cite_ref-twinstrivia.com_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twinstrivia.com-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> however MLB objected. Griffith therefore named the team the <i>Minnesota Twins</i>. However, the team was allowed to keep its original "TC" (for Twin Cities) insignia for its caps. The team\'s logo shows two men, one in a <a href="/wiki/Minneapolis_Millers" title="Minneapolis Millers">Minneapolis Millers</a> uniform and one in a <a href="/wiki/St._Paul_Saints_(1901%E2%80%9360)" title="St. Paul Saints (1901\xe2\x80\x9360)">St. Paul Saints</a> uniform, shaking hands across the <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_River" title="Mississippi River">Mississippi River</a> within an outline of the state of Minnesota. The "TC" remained on the Twins\' caps until 1987, when they adopted new uniforms. By this time, the team felt it was established enough to put an "M" on its cap without having St. Paul fans think it stood for Minneapolis. The "TC" logo was moved to a sleeve on the jerseys, and occasionally appeared as an alternate cap design.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Both the "TC" and "Minnie &amp; Paul" logos remain the team\'s primary insignia. As of 2010, the "TC" logo has been reinstated on the cap as their logo.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="1960s">1960s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Minnesota_Twins&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: 1960s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The Twins were eagerly greeted in Minnesota when they arrived in <a href="/wiki/1961_Minnesota_Twins_season" title="1961 Minnesota Twins season">1961</a>. They brought a nucleus of talented players: Harmon Killebrew, <a href="/wiki/Bob_Allison" title="Bob Allison">Bob Allison</a>, <a href="/wiki/Camilo_Pascual" title="Camilo Pascual">Camilo Pascual</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zoilo_Versalles" title="Zoilo Versalles">Zoilo Versalles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jim_Kaat" title="Jim Kaat">Jim Kaat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Earl_Battey" title="Earl Battey">Earl Battey</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lenny_Green" title="Lenny Green">Lenny Green</a>. <a href="/wiki/Tony_Oliva" title="Tony Oliva">Tony Oliva</a>, who would go on to win <a href="/wiki/List_of_Major_League_Baseball_batting_champions" title="List of Major League Baseball batting champions">American League batting championships</a> in <a href="/wiki/1964_Minnesota_Twins_season" title="1964 Minnesota Twins season">1964</a>, <a href="/wiki/1965_Minnesota_Twins_season" title="1965 Minnesota Twins season">1965</a> and <a href="/wiki/1971_Minnesota_Twins_season" title="1971 Minnesota Twins season">1971</a>, made his major league debut in <a href="/wiki/1962_Minnesota_Twins_season" title="1962 Minnesota Twins season">1962</a>.  That year, the Twins won 91 games, the most by the franchise since <a href="/wiki/1933_Washington_Senators_season" title="1933 Washington Senators season">1933</a>. Behind <a href="/wiki/Mudcat_Grant" title="Mudcat Grant">Mudcat Grant</a>\'s 21 victories, Versalles\' <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball_Most_Valuable_Player_Award" title="Major League Baseball Most Valuable Player Award">A.L. MVP season</a> and Oliva\'s batting title, the Twins won 102 games and the American League Pennant in <a href="/wiki/1965_Minnesota_Twins_season" title="1965 Minnesota Twins season">1965</a>, but they were defeated in the <a href="/wiki/1965_World_Series" title="1965 World Series">World Series</a> by the <a href="/wiki/1965_Los_Angeles_Dodgers_season" title="1965 Los Angeles Dodgers season">Los Angeles Dodgers</a> in seven games (behind the <a href="/wiki/World_Series_Most_Valuable_Player" class="mw-redirect" title="World Series Most Valuable Player">Series MVP</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sandy_Koufax" title="Sandy Koufax">Sandy Koufax</a>, who compiled a 2\xe2\x80\x931 record, including winning the seventh game).<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Heading into the final weekend of the <a href="/wiki/1967_Minnesota_Twins_season" title="1967 Minnesota Twins season">1967 season</a>, when <a href="/wiki/Rod_Carew" title="Rod Carew">Rod Carew</a> was named the <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball_Rookie_of_the_Year" class="mw-redirect" title="Major League Baseball Rookie of the Year">A.L. Rookie of the Year</a>, the Twins, <a href="/wiki/1967_Boston_Red_Sox_season" title="1967 Boston Red Sox season">Boston Red Sox</a>, <a href="/wiki/1967_Chicago_White_Sox_season" title="1967 Chicago White Sox season">Chicago White Sox</a>, and <a href="/wiki/1967_Detroit_Tigers_season" title="1967 Detroit Tigers season">Detroit Tigers</a> all had a shot at clinching the American League championship. The Twins and the Red Sox started the weekend tied for 1st place and played against each other in Boston for the final three games of the season. The Red Sox won two out of the three games, seizing their first pennant since 1946 with a 92\xe2\x80\x9370 record. The Twins and Tigers both finished one game back, with 91\xe2\x80\x9371 records, while the White Sox finished three games back, at 89\xe2\x80\x9373. In 1969, the new manager of the Twins, <a href="/wiki/Billy_Martin" title="Billy Martin">Billy Martin</a>, pushed aggressive base running all-around, and Carew set the all-time Major League record by stealing home seven times in addition to winning the first of seven <a href="/wiki/List_of_Major_League_Baseball_batting_champions" title="List of Major League Baseball batting champions">A.L. batting championships</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> With Killebrew slugging 49 homers and winning the <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball_Most_Valuable_Player_Award" title="Major League Baseball Most Valuable Player Award">AL MVP Award</a>, these <a href="/wiki/1969_Minnesota_Twins_season" title="1969 Minnesota Twins season">1969 Twins</a> won the very first <a href="/wiki/American_League_Western_Division" class="mw-redirect" title="American League Western Division">American League Western Division</a> Championship, but they lost three straight games to the <a href="/wiki/1969_Baltimore_Orioles_season" title="1969 Baltimore Orioles season">Baltimore Orioles</a>, winners of 109 games, in the first <a href="/wiki/1969_American_League_Championship_Series" title="1969 American League Championship Series">American League Championship Series</a>. The Orioles would go on to be upset by the <a href="/wiki/1969_New_York_Mets_season" title="1969 New York Mets season">New York Mets</a> in the <a href="/wiki/1969_World_Series" title="1969 World Series">World Series</a>. Martin was fired after the season, in part due to an August <a href="/wiki/1969_in_baseball" title="1969 in baseball">1969</a> fight in <a href="/wiki/Detroit" title="Detroit">Detroit</a> with 20-game winner <a href="/wiki/Dave_Boswell_(baseball)" title="Dave Boswell (baseball)">Dave Boswell</a> and outfielder <a href="/wiki/Bob_Allison" title="Bob Allison">Bob Allison</a>, in an alley outside the Lindell A.C. bar. <a href="/wiki/Bill_Rigney" title="Bill Rigney">Bill Rigney</a> led the Twins to a repeat division title in <a href="/wiki/1970_Minnesota_Twins_season" title="1970 Minnesota Twins season">1970</a>, behind the star pitching of Jim Perry (24-12), the A.L. <a href="/wiki/Cy_Young_Award" title="Cy Young Award">Cy Young Award</a> winner, while the Orioles again won the Eastern Division Championship behind the star pitching of <a href="/wiki/Jim_Palmer" title="Jim Palmer">Jim Palmer</a>. Once again, the Orioles won the <a href="/wiki/1970_American_League_Championship_Series" title="1970 American League Championship Series">A.L. Championship Series</a> in a three-game sweep,<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> and this time they would win the <a href="/wiki/1970_World_Series" title="1970 World Series">World Series</a>.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="1970s">1970s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Minnesota_Twins&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: 1970s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>After winning the division again in 1970, the team entered an eight-year dry spell, finishing around the .500 mark. Killebrew departed after 1974. Owner <a href="/wiki/Calvin_Griffith" title="Calvin Griffith">Calvin Griffith</a> faced financial difficulty with the start of <a href="/wiki/Free_agency" class="mw-redirect" title="Free agency">free agency</a>, costing the Twins the services of <a href="/wiki/Lyman_Bostock" title="Lyman Bostock">Lyman Bostock</a> and <a href="/wiki/Larry_Hisle" title="Larry Hisle">Larry Hisle</a>, who left as free agents after the 1977 season, and Carew, who was traded after the 1978 season.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> In 1975, Carew won his fourth consecutive AL batting title,<sup id="cite_ref-BR_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BR-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> having already joined <a href="/wiki/Ty_Cobb" title="Ty Cobb">Ty Cobb</a> as the only players to lead the major leagues in batting average for three consecutive seasons. In <a href="/wiki/1977_in_baseball" title="1977 in baseball">1977</a>, Carew batted .388, which was the highest in baseball since <a href="/wiki/Boston_Red_Sox" title="Boston Red Sox">Boston\'s</a> <a href="/wiki/Ted_Williams" title="Ted Williams">Ted Williams</a> hit .406 in <a href="/wiki/1941_in_baseball" title="1941 in baseball">1941</a>; he won the 1977 AL <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball_Most_Valuable_Player_Award" title="Major League Baseball Most Valuable Player Award">MVP Award</a>. He won another batting title in 1978, hitting .333.<sup id="cite_ref-BR_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BR-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span id="1980s.E2.80.9390s"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1980s\xe2\x80\x9390s">1980s\xe2\x80\x9390s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Minnesota_Twins&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: 1980s\xe2\x80\x9390s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:051207-MPLS-006Metrodome-crop.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/051207-MPLS-006Metrodome-crop.jpg/200px-051207-MPLS-006Metrodome-crop.jpg" width="200" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/051207-MPLS-006Metrodome-crop.jpg/300px-051207-MPLS-006Metrodome-crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/051207-MPLS-006Metrodome-crop.jpg/400px-051207-MPLS-006Metrodome-crop.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2024" data-file-height="1476" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:051207-MPLS-006Metrodome-crop.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Hubert_H._Humphrey_Metrodome" title="Hubert H. Humphrey Metrodome">Metrodome</a>, 2007</div></div></div>\n<p>In <a href="/wiki/1982_Minnesota_Twins_season" title="1982 Minnesota Twins season">1982</a>, the Twins moved into the <a href="/wiki/Hubert_H._Humphrey_Metrodome" title="Hubert H. Humphrey Metrodome">Hubert H. Humphrey Metrodome</a>, which they shared with the <a href="/wiki/Minnesota_Vikings" title="Minnesota Vikings">Minnesota Vikings</a>. After a 16-54 start, the Twins were on the verge on becoming the worst team in MLB history. They turned the season around somewhat, but still lost 102 games which is the second-worst record in Twins history (beaten only by the 2016 team, who lost 103 games), despite the .301 average, 23 homers and 92 RBI from rookie <a href="/wiki/Kent_Hrbek" title="Kent Hrbek">Kent Hrbek</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> In 1984, Griffith sold the Twins to multi-billionaire banker/financier <a href="/wiki/Carl_Pohlad" title="Carl Pohlad">Carl Pohlad</a>. The Metrodome hosted the <a href="/wiki/1985_Major_League_Baseball_All-Star_Game" title="1985 Major League Baseball All-Star Game">1985 Major League Baseball All-Star Game</a>. After several losing seasons, the <a href="/wiki/1987_Minnesota_Twins_season" title="1987 Minnesota Twins season">1987 team</a>, led by Hrbek, <a href="/wiki/Gary_Gaetti" title="Gary Gaetti">Gary Gaetti</a>, <a href="/wiki/Frank_Viola" title="Frank Viola">Frank Viola</a> (<a href="/wiki/Cy_Young_Award" title="Cy Young Award">A.L. Cy Young</a> winner in <a href="/wiki/1988_Minnesota_Twins_season" title="1988 Minnesota Twins season">1988</a>), <a href="/wiki/Bert_Blyleven" title="Bert Blyleven">Bert Blyleven</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jeff_Reardon" title="Jeff Reardon">Jeff Reardon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tom_Brunansky" title="Tom Brunansky">Tom Brunansky</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dan_Gladden" title="Dan Gladden">Dan Gladden</a>, and rising star <a href="/wiki/Kirby_Puckett" title="Kirby Puckett">Kirby Puckett</a>, returned to the World Series after defeating the favored <a href="/wiki/1987_Detroit_Tigers_season" title="1987 Detroit Tigers season">Detroit Tigers</a> in the <a href="/wiki/1987_American_League_Championship_Series" title="1987 American League Championship Series">ALCS</a>, 4 games to 1. <a href="/wiki/Tom_Kelly_(baseball)" title="Tom Kelly (baseball)">Tom Kelly</a> managed the Twins to World Series victories over the <a href="/wiki/1987_St._Louis_Cardinals_season" title="1987 St. Louis Cardinals season">St. Louis Cardinals</a> in <a href="/wiki/1987_World_Series" title="1987 World Series">1987</a><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/1991_Atlanta_Braves_season" title="1991 Atlanta Braves season">Atlanta Braves</a> in <a href="/wiki/1991_World_Series" title="1991 World Series">1991</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> The 1988 Twins were the first team in American League history to draw more than 3 million fans.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> On July 17, 1990, the Twins became the only team in major league history to pull off two triple plays in the same game.  Twins\' pitcher and Minnesota native <a href="/wiki/Jack_Morris" title="Jack Morris">Jack Morris</a> was the star of the series in 1991, going 2\xe2\x80\x930 in his three starts with a 1.17 ERA.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/1991_Minnesota_Twins_season" title="1991 Minnesota Twins season">1991</a> also marked the first time that any team that finished in last place in their division would advance to the World Series the following season; both the Twins and the Braves did this in 1991.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>  Contributors to the 1991 Twins\' improvement from 74 wins to 95 included <a href="/wiki/Chuck_Knoblauch" title="Chuck Knoblauch">Chuck Knoblauch</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball_Rookie_of_the_Year" class="mw-redirect" title="Major League Baseball Rookie of the Year">A.L. Rookie of the Year</a>; <a href="/wiki/Scott_Erickson" title="Scott Erickson">Scott Erickson</a>, 20-game winner; new closer <a href="/wiki/Rick_Aguilera" title="Rick Aguilera">Rick Aguilera</a> and new designated hitter <a href="/wiki/Chili_Davis" title="Chili Davis">Chili Davis</a>.\n</p><p>The World Series in 1991 is regarded by many as one of the classics of all time. In this Series, four games were won during the teams\' final at-bat, and three of these were in extra innings. The Atlanta Braves won all three of their games in Atlanta, and the Twins won all four of their games in Minnesota. The sixth game was a legendary one for Puckett, who tripled in a run, made a sensational leaping catch against the wall, and finally in the 11th inning hit the game-winning home run. The seventh game was tied 0\xe2\x80\x930 after the regulation nine innings, and marked only the second time that the seventh game of the World Series had ever gone into extra innings. The Twins won on a walk-off RBI single by <a href="/wiki/Gene_Larkin" title="Gene Larkin">Gene Larkin</a> in the bottom of the 10th inning, after Morris had pitched ten shutout innings against the Braves.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> The seventh game of the <a href="/wiki/1991_World_Series" title="1991 World Series">1991 World Series</a> is widely regarded as one of the greatest games in the history of professional baseball.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>After a winning season in <a href="/wiki/1992_Minnesota_Twins_season" title="1992 Minnesota Twins season">1992</a> but falling short of <a href="/wiki/1992_Oakland_Athletics_season" title="1992 Oakland Athletics season">Oakland</a> in the division, the Twins fell into a years-long stretch of mediocrity, posting a losing record each season for the next eight: 71\xe2\x80\x9391 in 1993, 50\xe2\x80\x9363 in 1994, 56\xe2\x80\x9388 in 1995, 78\xe2\x80\x9384 in 1996, 68\xe2\x80\x9394 in 1997, 70\xe2\x80\x9392 in 1998, 63\xe2\x80\x9397 in 1999 and 69\xe2\x80\x9393 in 2000. From 1994 to 1997, a long sequence of retirements and injuries hurt the team badly, and Tom Kelly spent the remainder of his managerial career attempting to rebuild the Twins. In 1997, owner <a href="/wiki/Carl_Pohlad" title="Carl Pohlad">Carl Pohlad</a> almost sold the Twins to <a href="/wiki/North_Carolina" title="North Carolina">North Carolina</a> businessman Don Beaver, who would have moved the team to the <a href="/wiki/Piedmont_Triad" title="Piedmont Triad">Piedmont Triad</a> area.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Puckett after the 1995 season was forced to retire at age 35 due to loss of vision in one eye from a <a href="/wiki/Central_retinal_vein_occlusion" title="Central retinal vein occlusion">central retinal vein occlusion</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/List_of_Major_League_Baseball_batting_champions" title="List of Major League Baseball batting champions">1989 A.L. batting champion</a>, he retired as the Twins\' all-time leader in career <a href="/wiki/Hit_(baseball)" title="Hit (baseball)">hits</a>, <a href="/wiki/Run_(baseball)" title="Run (baseball)">runs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Double_(baseball)" title="Double (baseball)">doubles</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Total_bases" title="Total bases">total bases</a>. At the time of his retirement, his .318 career <a href="/wiki/Batting_average" title="Batting average">batting average</a> was the highest by any right-handed <a href="/wiki/American_League" title="American League">American League</a> batter since <a href="/wiki/Joe_DiMaggio" title="Joe DiMaggio">Joe DiMaggio</a>. Puckett was the fourth baseball player during the 20th century to record 1,000 hits in his first five full calendar years in Major League Baseball, and was the second to record 2,000 hits during his first 10 full calendar years. He was elected to the <a href="/wiki/National_Baseball_Hall_of_Fame_and_Museum" title="National Baseball Hall of Fame and Museum">Baseball Hall of Fame</a> in <a href="/wiki/Baseball_Hall_of_Fame_balloting,_2001" title="Baseball Hall of Fame balloting, 2001">2001</a>, his first year of eligibility.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="2000s">2000s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Minnesota_Twins&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: 2000s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:00112696_Justin_Morneau.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/00112696_Justin_Morneau.jpg/200px-00112696_Justin_Morneau.jpg" width="200" height="201" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/00112696_Justin_Morneau.jpg/300px-00112696_Justin_Morneau.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/00112696_Justin_Morneau.jpg/400px-00112696_Justin_Morneau.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2573" data-file-height="2592" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:00112696_Justin_Morneau.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Justin_Morneau" title="Justin Morneau">Justin Morneau</a>, drafted in 1999 by the Twins, won the <a href="/wiki/AL_MVP" class="mw-redirect" title="AL MVP">AL MVP</a> award in 2006.</div></div></div>\n<p>The Twins dominated the Central Division in the first decade of the new century, winning the division in six of those ten years (\'02, \'03, \'04, \'06, \'09 and \'10), and nearly winning it in \'08 as well.  From 2001 to 2006, the Twins compiled the longest streak of consecutive winning seasons since moving to Minnesota.\n</p><p>Threatened with closure by <a href="#Contraction">league contraction</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/2002_Minnesota_Twins_season" title="2002 Minnesota Twins season">2002 team</a> battled back to reach the <a href="/wiki/2002_American_League_Championship_Series" title="2002 American League Championship Series">American League Championship Series</a> before being eliminated 4\xe2\x80\x931 by that year\'s World Series champion Anaheim Angels.  The Twins have not won a playoff series since the 2002 series against the Athletics, this despite the team winning several division championships in the decade.  \n</p>\n<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="2006">2006</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Minnesota_Twins&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: 2006">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/2006_Minnesota_Twins_season" title="2006 Minnesota Twins season">2006 Minnesota Twins season</a></div>\n<p>In 2006, the Twins won the division on the last day of the regular season (the only day all season they held sole possession of first place) but lost to the Oakland Athletics in the <a href="/wiki/2006_American_League_Division_Series" title="2006 American League Division Series">ALDS</a>. <a href="/wiki/Ozzie_Guill%C3%A9n" title="Ozzie Guill\xc3\xa9n">Ozzie Guill\xc3\xa9n</a> coined a nickname for this squad, calling the Twins "<a href="/wiki/Piranhas_(baseball)" title="Piranhas (baseball)">little piranhas</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> The Twins players embraced the label, and in response, the Twins Front office started a "Piranha Night", with piranha finger puppets given out to the first 10,000 fans. Scoreboard operators sometimes played an animated sequence of piranhas munching under that caption in situations where the Twins were scoring runs playing "<a href="/wiki/Small_ball_(baseball)" title="Small ball (baseball)">small ball</a>", and the stadium vendors sold T-shirts and hats advertising "The Little Piranhas".\n</p><p>The Twins also had the AL MVP in Justin Morneau,<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> the AL batting champion in <a href="/wiki/Joe_Mauer" title="Joe Mauer">Joe Mauer</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> and the AL Cy Young Award winner in <a href="/wiki/Johan_Santana" title="Johan Santana">Johan Santana</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="2008">2008</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Minnesota_Twins&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: 2008">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/2008_Minnesota_Twins_season" title="2008 Minnesota Twins season">2008 Minnesota Twins season</a></div>\n<p>In 2008, the Twins finished the regular season tied with the White Sox on top of the AL Central, forcing a <a href="/wiki/2008_American_League_Central_tie-breaker_game" title="2008 American League Central tie-breaker game">one-game playoff</a> in Chicago to determine the division champion.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> The Twins lost that game and missed the playoffs. The game location was determined by rule of a coin flip that was conducted in mid-September. This rule was changed for the start of the 2009 season, making the site for any <a href="/wiki/One-game_playoff" title="One-game playoff">tiebreaker game</a> to be determined by the winner of the regular season head-to-head record between the teams involved.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="2009">2009</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Minnesota_Twins&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: 2009">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/2009_Minnesota_Twins_season" title="2009 Minnesota Twins season">2009 Minnesota Twins season</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:0923_493c_Joe_Nathan.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/0923_493c_Joe_Nathan.jpg/200px-0923_493c_Joe_Nathan.jpg" width="200" height="157" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/0923_493c_Joe_Nathan.jpg/300px-0923_493c_Joe_Nathan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/0923_493c_Joe_Nathan.jpg/400px-0923_493c_Joe_Nathan.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2051" data-file-height="1605" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:0923_493c_Joe_Nathan.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Joe_Nathan" title="Joe Nathan">Joe Nathan</a> won the <a href="/wiki/Rolaids_Relief_Man_Award" title="Rolaids Relief Man Award">Rolaids Relief Man Award</a> in 2009.</div></div></div>\n<p>After a year where the Twins played .500 baseball for most of the season, the team won 17 of their last 21 games to tie the <a href="/wiki/Detroit_Tigers" title="Detroit Tigers">Detroit Tigers</a> for the lead in the Central Division.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> The Twins were able to use the play-in game rule to their advantage when they won the <a href="/wiki/American_League_Central" title="American League Central">AL Central</a> at the end of the regular season by way of a 6\xe2\x80\x935 <a href="/wiki/2009_American_League_Central_tie-breaker_game" title="2009 American League Central tie-breaker game">tiebreaker game</a> that concluded with a 12th-inning walk-off hit by <a href="/wiki/Alexi_Casilla" title="Alexi Casilla">Alexi Casilla</a> to right field, that scored <a href="/wiki/Carlos_G%C3%B3mez" title="Carlos G\xc3\xb3mez">Carlos G\xc3\xb3mez</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> However, they failed to advance to the <a href="/wiki/2009_American_League_Championship_Series" title="2009 American League Championship Series">American League Championship Series</a> as they lost the <a href="/wiki/2009_American_League_Championship_Series" title="2009 American League Championship Series">American League Divisional Series</a> in three straight games to the eventual World Series champion New York Yankees.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> That year, Joe Mauer became only the second catcher in 33 years to win the AL MVP award.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Iv%C3%A1n_Rodr%C3%ADguez" title="Iv\xc3\xa1n Rodr\xc3\xadguez">Iv\xc3\xa1n Rodr\xc3\xadguez</a> won for the <a href="/wiki/Texas_Rangers_(baseball)" title="Texas Rangers (baseball)">Texas Rangers</a> in 1999, previous to that, the last catcher to win an AL MVP was the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Yankees" title="New York Yankees">New York Yankees</a> <a href="/wiki/Thurman_Munson" title="Thurman Munson">Thurman Munson</a> in 1976.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="2010">2010</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Minnesota_Twins&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: 2010">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/2010_Minnesota_Twins_season" title="2010 Minnesota Twins season">2010 Minnesota Twins season</a></div>\n<p>In their inaugural season played at <a href="/wiki/Target_Field" title="Target Field">Target Field</a>, the Twins finished the regular season with a record of 94-68, clinching the AL Central Division title for the 6th time in 9 years under manager <a href="/wiki/Ron_Gardenhire" title="Ron Gardenhire">Ron Gardenhire</a>. New regular players included rookie <a href="/wiki/Danny_Valencia" title="Danny Valencia">Danny Valencia</a> at third base, designated hitter <a href="/wiki/Jim_Thome" title="Jim Thome">Jim Thome</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> closer <a href="/wiki/Matt_Capps" title="Matt Capps">Matt Capps</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> infielder <a href="/wiki/J._J._Hardy" title="J. J. Hardy">J. J. Hardy</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> and infielder <a href="/wiki/Orlando_Hudson" title="Orlando Hudson">Orlando Hudson</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> In relief pitching roles were late additions <a href="/wiki/Brian_Fuentes" title="Brian Fuentes">Brian Fuentes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Randy_Flores" title="Randy Flores">Randy Flores</a>. On July 7, the team suffered a major blow when Justin Morneau sustained a concussion, which knocked him out for the rest of the season. In the <a href="/wiki/2010_American_League_Division_Series" title="2010 American League Division Series">divisional series</a>, the Twins lost to the Yankees in a three-game sweep for the second consecutive year.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> Following the season, Ron Gardenhire received AL <a href="/wiki/Manager_of_the_Year_Award" class="mw-redirect" title="Manager of the Year Award">Manager of the Year</a> honors after finishing as a runner up in several prior years.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="2011">2011</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Minnesota_Twins&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: 2011">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/2011_Minnesota_Twins_season" title="2011 Minnesota Twins season">2011 Minnesota Twins season</a></div>\n<p>After repeating as AL Central champions in 2010, the Twins entered 2011 with no players on the disabled list, and the team seemed poised for another strong season. During the off-season, the team signed Japanese shortstop <a href="/wiki/Tsuyoshi_Nishioka" title="Tsuyoshi Nishioka">Tsuyoshi Nishioka</a> to fill a hole in the middle infield, re-signed Jim Thome, who was in pursuit of career home run number 600, and also re-signed <a href="/wiki/Carl_Pavano" title="Carl Pavano">Carl Pavano</a>. However, the season was largely derailed by an extensive list of injuries. Nishioka\'s broken leg in a collision at second base<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> led the way and was followed by DL stints from <a href="/wiki/Kevin_Slowey" title="Kevin Slowey">Kevin Slowey</a>, Joe Mauer, <a href="/wiki/Jason_Repko" title="Jason Repko">Jason Repko</a>, Thome, <a href="/wiki/Delmon_Young" title="Delmon Young">Delmon Young</a> (two stints on the DL), <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Mijares" title="Jos\xc3\xa9 Mijares">Jos\xc3\xa9 Mijares</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glen_Perkins" title="Glen Perkins">Glen Perkins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Joe_Nathan" title="Joe Nathan">Joe Nathan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Francisco_Liriano" title="Francisco Liriano">Francisco Liriano</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jason_Kubel" title="Jason Kubel">Jason Kubel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Denard_Span" title="Denard Span">Denard Span</a> (two stints), Justin Morneau, <a href="/wiki/Scott_Baker_(right-handed_pitcher)" title="Scott Baker (right-handed pitcher)">Scott Baker</a>, and Alexi Casilla. The team\'s low point was arguably on May 1 when the team started 7 players who were batting below .235 in a game against Kansas City.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> From that day forward, the Twins made a strong push to get as close as five games back of the division lead by the All-Star break. However, the team struggled down the stretch and fell back out of contention. The team failed to reach the playoffs for the first time since 2008 and experienced their first losing season in four years. Despite an AL-worst 63-99 record, the team drew over 3 million fans for the second consecutive year.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Michael_Cuddyer" title="Michael Cuddyer">Michael Cuddyer</a> served as the Twins representative at the All-Star game, his first appearance.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Bert Blyleven\'s number was retired during the season and he was also inducted into the Baseball Hall of Fame during the month of July.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> On August 10, Nathan recorded his 255th save, passing <a href="/wiki/Rick_Aguilera" title="Rick Aguilera">Rick Aguilera</a> for first place on the franchise\'s all-time saves list.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> On August 15, Thome hit 599th and 600th home run at <a href="/wiki/Comerica_Park" title="Comerica Park">Comerica Park</a> to become the eighth player in Major League history to hit 600 home runs, joining <a href="/wiki/Babe_Ruth" title="Babe Ruth">Babe Ruth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Willie_Mays" title="Willie Mays">Willie Mays</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hank_Aaron" title="Hank Aaron">Hank Aaron</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barry_Bonds" title="Barry Bonds">Barry Bonds</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sammy_Sosa" title="Sammy Sosa">Sammy Sosa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ken_Griffey,_Jr." class="mw-redirect" title="Ken Griffey, Jr.">Ken Griffey, Jr.</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Alex_Rodriguez" title="Alex Rodriguez">Alex Rodriguez</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h5><span id="2012.E2.80.932016"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="2012\xe2\x80\x932016">2012\xe2\x80\x932016</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Minnesota_Twins&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: 2012\xe2\x80\x932016">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/2012_Minnesota_Twins_season" title="2012 Minnesota Twins season">2012 Minnesota Twins season</a>, <a href="/wiki/2013_Minnesota_Twins_season" title="2013 Minnesota Twins season">2013 Minnesota Twins season</a>, <a href="/wiki/2014_Minnesota_Twins_season" title="2014 Minnesota Twins season">2014 Minnesota Twins season</a>, and <a href="/wiki/2015_Minnesota_Twins_season" title="2015 Minnesota Twins season">2015 Minnesota Twins season</a></div>\n<p>The team started the 2012 season with a league worst 10-24 record.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> In late May and early June, the team embarked on a hot streak, winning ten out of thirteen games.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> By mid July, the team found themselves only 10 games out of the division lead.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> On July 16, the Twins defeated the <a href="/wiki/Baltimore_Orioles" title="Baltimore Orioles">Baltimore Orioles</a> 19-7, the most runs scored in the short history of Target Field.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> By the end of August, the Twins were more than 20 games below .500, and last in the American League.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> On August 29, it was announced that the Twins would host the <a href="/wiki/2014_Major_League_Baseball_All-Star_Game" title="2014 Major League Baseball All-Star Game">2014 All-Star Game</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> In 2013, the Twins finished in 4th place in the <a href="/wiki/American_League_Central_Division" class="mw-redirect" title="American League Central Division">AL Central</a>, with a record of 66-96.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> In 2014, the team finished with a 70-92 record, last in the division and accumulated the second fewest wins in the American League.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> As a result, <a href="/wiki/Ron_Gardenhire" title="Ron Gardenhire">Ron Gardenhire</a> was fired on September 29, 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> On November 3, 2014 <a href="/wiki/Paul_Molitor" title="Paul Molitor">Paul Molitor</a> was announced by the team as the 13th manager in Twins history.\n</p><p>In 2015, the team had a winning season (83-79), following four consecutive seasons of 90 or more losses.\n</p><p>In 2016, the Minnesota Twins finished last in the AL Central, with a 59-103 record. <a href="/wiki/Brian_Dozier" title="Brian Dozier">Brian Dozier</a> set his career high in home runs with 43, which was tied for second in baseball, and leading all 2nd basemen. <a href="/wiki/Tyler_Duffey" title="Tyler Duffey">Tyler Duffey</a> led all Twins starters with 9 wins throughout the season, while fellow reliever <a href="/wiki/Brandon_Kintzler" title="Brandon Kintzler">Brandon Kintzler</a> led the team with 17 saves. Rising stars <a href="/wiki/Miguel_Sano" class="mw-redirect" title="Miguel Sano">Miguel Sano</a>, <a href="/wiki/Max_Kepler" title="Max Kepler">Max Kepler</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Byron_Buxton" title="Byron Buxton">Byron Buxton</a> combined to have 263 total hits, 52 home runs, 167 RBIs, and a batting average of .232 throughout the season. The Twins signed star Korean slugger <a href="/wiki/Byung_Ho_Park" class="mw-redirect" title="Byung Ho Park">Byung Ho Park</a> to a 4-year/$12 million contract, where he hit a .191 batting average, with 12 home runs, and 24 RBIs before being sent down to Rochester for the remainder of the season.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Threatened_contraction_or_relocation_of_the_team">Threatened contraction or relocation of the team</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Minnesota_Twins&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Threatened contraction or relocation of the team">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Metrodome_Twins.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Metrodome_Twins.jpg/200px-Metrodome_Twins.jpg" width="200" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Metrodome_Twins.jpg/300px-Metrodome_Twins.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Metrodome_Twins.jpg/400px-Metrodome_Twins.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="960" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Metrodome_Twins.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Hubert_H._Humphrey_Metrodome" title="Hubert H. Humphrey Metrodome">Metrodome</a> in 2006.</div></div></div>\n<p>The quirks of the <a href="/wiki/Hubert_H._Humphrey_Metrodome" title="Hubert H. Humphrey Metrodome">Hubert H. Humphrey Metrodome</a>, including the turf floor and the white roof, gave the Twins a significant <a href="/wiki/Home-field_advantage" class="mw-redirect" title="Home-field advantage">home-field advantage</a> that played into their winning the World Series in both 1987 and 1991, at least in the opinion of their opponents, as the Twins went 12\xe2\x80\x931 in postseason home games during those two seasons.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> These were the first two World Series in professional baseball history in which a team won the championship by winning all four home games.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> (The feat has since been repeated once, by the <a href="/wiki/2001_World_Series" title="2001 World Series">Arizona Diamondbacks in 2001</a>.) Nevertheless, the Twins argued that the Metrodome was obsolete and that the lack of a dedicated baseball-only ballpark limited team revenue and made it difficult to sustain a top-notch, competitive team (the Twins had been sharing tenancy in stadiums with the <a href="/wiki/National_Football_League" title="National Football League">NFL</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Minnesota_Vikings" title="Minnesota Vikings">Minnesota Vikings</a> since 1961). The team was rumored to contemplate moving to such places as <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey" title="New Jersey">New Jersey</a>, Las Vegas, <a href="/wiki/Portland,_Oregon" title="Portland, Oregon">Portland, Oregon</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Greensboro,_North_Carolina" title="Greensboro, North Carolina">Greensboro</a>/<a href="/wiki/Winston-Salem,_North_Carolina" title="Winston-Salem, North Carolina">Winston-Salem, North Carolina</a>, area, and elsewhere in search of a more financially competitive market. In 2002, the team was nearly disbanded when Major League Baseball selected the Twins and the <a href="/wiki/Montreal_Expos" title="Montreal Expos">Montreal Expos</a> (now the <a href="/wiki/Washington_Nationals" title="Washington Nationals">Washington Nationals</a> franchise) for elimination due to their financial weakness relative to other franchises in the league. The impetus for league contraction diminished after a court decision forced the Twins to play out their lease on the Metrodome. However, Twins owner Carl Pohlad continued his efforts to relocate, pursuing litigation against the Metropolitan Stadium Commission and obtaining a state court ruling that his team was not obligated to play in the Metrodome after the 2006 season. This cleared the way for the Twins to either be relocated or disbanded prior to the 2007 season if a new deal was not reached.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Target_Field">Target Field</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Minnesota_Twins&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Target Field">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Target_Field" title="Target Field">Target Field</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Target_Field_April_2010.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Target_Field_April_2010.jpg/200px-Target_Field_April_2010.jpg" width="200" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Target_Field_April_2010.jpg/300px-Target_Field_April_2010.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Target_Field_April_2010.jpg/400px-Target_Field_April_2010.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3648" data-file-height="2736" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Target_Field_April_2010.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Target_Field" title="Target Field">Target Field</a> in 2010.</div></div></div>\n<p>In response to the threatened loss of the Twins, the Minnesota private and public sector negotiated and approved a financing package for a replacement stadium\xe2\x80\x94 a baseball-only outdoor, natural turf ballpark in the <a href="/wiki/North_Loop,_Minneapolis" title="North Loop, Minneapolis">Warehouse District</a> of <a href="/wiki/Central,_Minneapolis" title="Central, Minneapolis">downtown Minneapolis</a>\xe2\x80\x94 owned by a new entity known as the Minnesota Ballpark Authority.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>  <a href="/wiki/Target_Field" title="Target Field">Target Field</a> was constructed at a cost of $544.4&#160;million (including site acquisition and infrastructure), utilizing the proceeds of a $392&#160;million public bond offering based on a 0.15% sales tax in Hennepin County and private financing of $185&#160;million provided by the <a href="/wiki/Carl_Pohlad" title="Carl Pohlad">Pohlad family</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>  As part of the deal, the Twins also signed a 30-year lease of the new stadium, effectively guaranteeing the continuation of the team in Minnesota for a long time to come. Construction of the new field began in 2007, and was completed in December 2009, in time for the 2010 season. Commissioner <a href="/wiki/Bud_Selig" title="Bud Selig">Bud Selig</a>, who earlier had threatened to disband the team, observed that without the new stadium the Twins could not have committed to sign their star player, catcher <a href="/wiki/Joe_Mauer" title="Joe Mauer">Joe Mauer</a>, to an 8-year, $184&#160;million contract extension. The first regular season game in Target Field was played against the <a href="/wiki/Boston_Red_Sox" title="Boston Red Sox">Boston Red Sox</a> on April 12, 2010, with Mauer driving in two runs and going 3-for-5 to help the Twins defeat the Red Sox, 5\xe2\x80\x932.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On May 18, 2011, Target Field was named "The Best Place To Shop" by <a href="/wiki/Street_and_Smith" class="mw-redirect" title="Street and Smith">Street and Smith</a>\'s <i>SportsBusiness Journal</i> at the magazine\'s 2011 Sports Business Awards Ceremony in New York City.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> It was also named "The Best Sports Stadium in North America" by <i><a href="/wiki/ESPN_The_Magazine" title="ESPN The Magazine">ESPN The Magazine</a></i> in a ranking that included over 120 different stadiums, ballparks and arenas from around North America.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In July 2014, Target Field hosted the <a href="/wiki/2014_Major_League_Baseball_All-Star_Game" title="2014 Major League Baseball All-Star Game">85th Major League Baseball All-Star Game</a> and the <a href="/wiki/2014_Major_League_Baseball_Home_Run_Derby" title="2014 Major League Baseball Home Run Derby">Home Run Derby</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Current_roster">Current roster</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Minnesota_Twins&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Current roster">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<table
